Prime Minister <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Serbia>
before 	
Ana Brnabić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ana_Brnabi%C4%87>
SNS <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serbian_Progressive_Party> 	

Parliamentary elections were held in Serbia <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Serbia> on 17 December 2023 to elect members of the National
Assembly <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Assembly_(Serbia)>.
While they were initially scheduled to be held by 30 April 2026,
Aleksandar Vučić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Aleksandar_Vu%C4%8Di%C4%87>, the president of Serbia, called a snap
election <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Snap_election> in November 2023,
after previously announcing that snap elections could be either held in
2023 or 2024. In addition to the parliamentary elections, the Vojvodina
provincial <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
2023_Vojvodina_provincial_election> and local <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/2023_Serbian_local_elections> elections were held in 65 cities and
municipalities, including the capital, Belgrade <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2023_Belgrade_City_Assembly_election>.

The Serbian Progressive Party <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Serbian_Progressive_Party> (SNS) came to power after the 2012 election
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2012_Serbian_parliamentary_election> when
it formed a coalition government with the Socialist Party of Serbia
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Socialist_Party_of_Serbia>. In the 2022
parliamentary election <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
2022_Serbian_general_election>, SNS lost its parliamentary majority
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parliamentary_majority> while opposition
parties returned to the National Assembly. The United for the Victory of
Serbia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_for_the_Victory_of_Serbia>
alliance, which placed second, was dissolved shortly after the election.
Ana Brnabić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ana_Brnabi%C4%87>, who has
been the prime minister <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Prime_Minister_of_Serbia> since 2017, and her third cabinet <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_cabinet_of_Ana_Brnabi%C4%87> were
inaugurated in October 2022. Her cabinet saw several changes in 2023;
Branko Ružić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Branko_Ru%C5%BEi%C4%87_(politician)> resigned and Rade Basta <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rade_Basta> was dismissed. Brnabić's cabinet has
also been involved in the North Kosovo crisis <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/North_Kosovo_crisis_(2022%E2%80%93present)> and was faced with
anti-government protests <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
2023_Serbian_protests> from May to November 2023, which were triggered
after the Belgrade school shooting <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Belgrade_school_shooting> and a mass murder near Mladenovac and
Smederevo <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Mladenovac_and_Smederevo_shootings>.

Opposition parties organising the protests formed the Serbia Against
Violence <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Serbia_Against_Violence_(coalition)> coalition in October. The campaign
was met with an increase in political tensions, polarisation <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Political_polarization>, and voter intimidation
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voter_intimidation>. Candidates
campaigned on issues such as fighting against crime and corruption,
decreasing inflation, and the Ohrid Agreement <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Ohrid_Agreement_(2023)>. Despite not being a candidate and no
longer being president of SNS, Vučić mainly represented SNS during the
campaign. The Republic Electoral Commission proclaimed 18 electoral
lists for the parliamentary election.

Monitoring and non-governmental organisations reported that the election
day was marked with electoral fraud <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Electoral_fraud>, mostly in the Belgrade <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Belgrade> region. ODIHR <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ODIHR> concluded
that the elections were well organised, but that SNS had a systematic
advantage in the election and abused public funds. The election resulted
into SNS regaining its parliamentary majority, despite opinion polls
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Opinion_polling_for_the_2023_Serbian_parliamentary_election> predicting
a decrease of support, and SPS suffering from its worst result since the
2007 election <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
2007_Serbian_parliamentary_election>. The We–The Voice from the People
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/We%E2%80%93The_Voice_from_the_People> of
conspiracy theorist Branimir Nestorović <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Branimir_Nestorovi%C4%87> also gained representation in the National
Assembly. Mass protests <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
2023_Serbian_election_protests> began a day after the election.


    Background

A populist <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Populist> coalition, led by
the Serbian Progressive Party <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Serbian_Progressive_Party> (SNS), came to power after the 2012 election
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2012_Serbian_parliamentary_election>,
along with the Socialist Party of Serbia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Socialist_Party_of_Serbia> (SPS).^[1] <#cite_note-1> ^[2] <#cite_note-2>
Aleksandar Vučić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Aleksandar_Vu%C4%8Di%C4%87>, who initially served as deputy prime
minister <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deputy_Prime_Minister_of_Serbia>
and later as prime minister <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Prime_Minister_of_Serbia>, was elected president <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_Serbia> in 2017 <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2017_Serbian_presidential_election> and re-elected
in 2022 <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
2022_Serbian_general_election>.^[3] <#cite_note-3> ^[4] <#cite_note-4>
Since he came to power, observers have assessed that Serbia has suffered
from democratic backsliding <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Democratic_backsliding> into authoritarianism <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Authoritarianism>, followed by a decline in media freedom <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Media_freedom_in_Serbia> and civil liberties
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Civil_liberties>.^[5] <#cite_note-5> ^[6]
<#cite_note-6> In 2023, the V-Dem Institute <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/V-Dem_Institute> categorised Serbia as an electoral autocracy
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electoral_autocracy>, while Freedom House
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Freedom_House> noted that SNS "eroded
political rights and civil liberties, put pressure on independent media,
the opposition, and civil society organisations".^[7] <#cite_note-7>
^[8] <#cite_note-8> ^[9] <#cite_note-9>

The Together We Can Do Everything <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Together_We_Can_Do_Everything> coalition, which was led by SNS, lost its
parliamentary majority <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Parliamentary_majority> in the 2022 parliamentary election, but the
election also oversaw twelve electoral lists in total, including the
ones from the opposition, that crossed the 3 percent threshold <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electoral_threshold>.^[10] <#cite_note-10> Non-
governmental and election monitoring organisations reported that
electoral irregularities <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Election_irregularities> occurred during the voting day.^[11]
<#cite_note-11> ^[12] <#cite_note-12> ^[13] <#cite_note-13> Following
the election, the United for the Victory of Serbia <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_for_the_Victory_of_Serbia> (UZPS)
coalition, which placed second, was dissolved.^[14] <#cite_note-:1-14>
^[15] <#cite_note-15>

Ana Brnabić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ana_Brnabi%C4%87>, who has
been the prime minister <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Prime_Minister_of_Serbia> since 2017, and her third cabinet <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_cabinet_of_Ana_Brnabi%C4%87> were sworn in
on 26 October 2022.^[16] <#cite_note-16> Rade Basta <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rade_Basta>, a minister in Brnabić's cabinet,
voiced his support for introducing sanctions on Russia <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
International_sanctions_during_the_Russian_invasion_of_Ukraine> in March
2023.^[17] <#cite_note-17> SPS and United Serbia <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_Serbia> (JS) later submitted a proposal to
dismiss him from government, which went successful.^[18] <#cite_note-:
53-18> ^[19] <#cite_note-19> In May 2023, Vučić was succeeded by Miloš
Vučević <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milo%C5%A1_Vu%C4%8Devi%C4%87> as
president of SNS.^[20] <#cite_note-20>


      North Kosovo crisis

Two trucks roadblocking in North Kosovo <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:North_Kosovo_crisis_2022_barricades.jpg>
Tensions between Serbia and Kosovo escalated in July 2022

Beginning in July 2022, tensions between Serbia and Kosovo <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kosovo> heightened, firstly due to the expiration
of the eleven-year validity period for car documents.^[21]
<#cite_note-21> ^[22] <#cite_note-:20-22> After negotiations with the
diplomatic representatives of the European Union <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Union> in August, an agreement on car
documents was reached.^[23] <#cite_note-23> The agreement went into
effect on 1 September, with the deadline for obtaining new car documents
set for 31 October 2022.^[22] <#cite_note-:20-22> A proposed agreement
to normalise tensions, later-informally known as the Ohrid Agreement
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ohrid_Agreement_(2023)>, was sent to
Serbia and Kosovo by Jens Plötner <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=Jens_Pl%C3%B6tner&action=edit&redlink=1> [de <https://
de.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jens_Pl%C3%B6tner>] and Emmanuel Bonne <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Emmanuel_Bonne> and was leaked in September
2022.^[24] <#cite_note-24> Its existence was confirmed by Serbia and
Kosovo a month later.^[25] <#cite_note-25> Shortly before the deadline
for the car documents expired, Albin Kurti <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Albin_Kurti>, the prime minister of Kosovo, announced that a phased
implementation would instead be implemented up to 21 April 2023.^[26]
<#cite_note-26> In protest to the announcement, hundreds of Kosovo Serb
police officers, mayors, and Serb List <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Serb_List_(Kosovo)> politicians resigned from their positions.^[27]
<#cite_note-:92-27> Another agreement was reached in November 2022,
after negotiations with the European Union; it was announced that
licence plates issued by Serbia would continue to be in use in North
Kosovo.^[28] <#cite_note-:132-28> ^[29] <#cite_note-29>

After claims that the Regional Operational Support Unit (ROSU) allegedly
raided North Mitrovica <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Mitrovica>
in December 2022, Serbia announced that it would deploy Serbian forces
to Kosovo.^[30] <#cite_note-:152-30> The government of Kosovo <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Government_of_Kosovo> rejected the claim that ROSU
entered North Mitrovica, while Kosovo Force <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Kosovo_Force> rejected Serbia's request to deploy Serbian forces to
Kosovo.^[31] <#cite_note-31> ^[32] <#cite_note-32> Amidst this,
barricades were put up in North Kosovo and ultranationalist <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ultranationalist> protests were organised in
Belgrade.^[33] <#cite_note-:19-33> ^[34] <#cite_note-34> Negotiations
between Serbia and Kosovo continued, and in March 2023 Vučić and Kurti
verbally agreed to implement the Ohrid Agreement.^[35] <#cite_note-35>

An attack perpetrated by Serb militants <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Militants> occurred on 24 September 2023 in Banjska <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Banjska_(village)>, a village in North Kosovo
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/North_Kosovo>.^[36] <#cite_note-:21-36>
^[37] <#cite_note-37> The attack resulted in death of one Kosovo Police
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kosovo_Police> officer and three Serb
militants.^[36] <#cite_note-:21-36> Milan Radoičić <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milan_Radoi%C4%8Di%C4%87>, a businessman and
member of the Serb List, admitted being involved in the attack; he was
later arrested by the government of Serbia, but the Supreme Court
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supreme_Court_(Serbia)> denied the motion
to place Radoičić in custody for 30 days and he was ultimately
released.^[38] <#cite_note-38> ^[39] <#cite_note-39> In response to the
attack, Serbia declared a national day of mourning <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_day_of_mourning> for 27 September due to
the death of three Serb militants.^[40] <#cite_note-40> Kosovo alleged
that the Serb participants had close relations with Vučić and the
government of Serbia.^[41] <#cite_note-41> ^[42] <#cite_note-42> The
European Parliament <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/European_Parliament>
also adopted a resolution to introduce measures against Serbia if proven
to be directly involved in the attack.^[43] <#cite_note-43>


      2023 protests

Demonstrators protesting in the Kneza Miloša Street on 19 May 2023
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
File:Srbija_protiv_nasilja,_19._maj_7_(cropped).jpg>
Demonstrators on 19 May 2023

In May 2023, the Belgrade school shooting <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Belgrade_school_shooting> and a mass murder near Mladenovac and
Smederevo <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Mladenovac_and_Smederevo_shootings> occurred.^[44] <#cite_note-44> ^[45]
<#cite_note-45> The government responded by adopting measures such as
stricter regulations on gun ownership and hiring 1,200 police officers
to schools.^[46] <#cite_note-bbcnews1-46> ^[47] <#cite_note-47> It was
also criticised, particularly due to the statement of Branko Ružić
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Branko_Ru%C5%BEi%C4%87_(politician)>, the
minister of education, who said that "a cancerous, pernicious influence
of the Internet, video games, and so-called Western values, is evident"
in the shooting, and Brnabić, who said that the "system did not fail"
when responding to the claims that the government could have stopped the
shootings.^[48] <#cite_note-48> ^[49] <#cite_note-49>

This resulted in mass protests, named Serbia Against Violence, which
began on 8 May.^[50] <#cite_note-:2-50> ^[51] <#cite_note-51> Tens of
thousands attended the protests.^[52] <#cite_note-:6-52> ^[53]
<#cite_note-53> ^[54] <#cite_note-54> Despite being organised by the
Democratic Party <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Democratic_Party_(Serbia)> (DS), Do not let Belgrade drown <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Do_not_let_Belgrade_drown> (NDB), Party of Freedom
and Justice <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Party_of_Freedom_and_Justice>
(SSP), People's Party <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
People%27s_Party_(Serbia,_2017)> (Narodna), and Together <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Together_(Serbia)> opposition parties, no party
signs were reported to be seen at the protests.^[55] <#cite_note-:8-55>
In response to Serbia Against Violence protests, Vučić held an SNS-
organised gathering on 26 May.^[56] <#cite_note-56> A number of farmers
also protested from 16 to 20 May.^[57] <#cite_note-:9-57> ^[58]
<#cite_note-58> The protests have been continuously organised in
Belgrade and other cities from May 2023^[59] <#cite_note-59> ^[60]
<#cite_note-60> ^[61] <#cite_note-61> until November 2023.^[62]
<#cite_note-62>

In response to the shootings, and a day before the protests began, Ružić
announced his resignation.^[63] <#cite_note-63> Đorđe Milićević
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C4%90or%C4%91e_Mili%C4%87evi%C4%87> was
appointed as acting minister and was succeeded by Slavica Đukić
Dejanović <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Slavica_%C4%90uki%C4%87_Dejanovi%C4%87> in late July 2023.^[64]
<#cite_note-milicevicvd-64> ^[65] <#cite_note-dejanovic-65> As one of
the demands of the protests, organisers initiated a motion in the
National Assembly to dismiss minister Bratislav Gašić <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bratislav_Ga%C5%A1i%C4%87> in early July 2023,
however, the motion was unsuccessful.^[66] <#cite_note-66>


    Electoral system

The 250 members of the National Assembly <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
National_Assembly_(Serbia)> are elected by closed-list <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Closed-list> proportional representation <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proportional_representation> from a single
nationwide constituency <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Constituency>.^[67] <#cite_note-:02-67> ^[68] <#cite_note-:0-68>
Eligible voters vote for electoral lists <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Electoral_list>, on which the candidates of the accepted lists are
present.^[68] <#cite_note-:0-68> A maximum of 250 candidates could be
present on a single electoral list.^[68] <#cite_note-:0-68> An electoral
list could be submitted by a registered political party, a coalition of
political parties, or a citizens' group.^[68] <#cite_note-:0-68> To
submit an electoral list, at least 10,000 valid signatures must be
collected, though ethnic minority <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Minority_group> parties only need to collect 5,000 signatures to qualify
on ballot.^[68] <#cite_note-:0-68> ^[69] <#cite_note-:5-69> At least 40
percent of candidates on electoral lists must be female.^[70]
<#cite_note-70> The electoral list is submitted by its chosen
representative or representatives.^[71] <#cite_note-:3-71> An electoral
list could be declined, after which those who had submitted can fix the
deficiencies in a span of 48 hours, or rejected, if the person is not
authorised to nominate candidates.^[71] <#cite_note-:3-71> The name and
date of the election, names of the electoral lists and its
representatives, and information on how to vote are only present on the
voting ballot.^[71] <#cite_note-:3-71>

The Republic Electoral Commission <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=Republic_Electoral_Commission_(Serbia)&action=edit&redlink=1>
(RIK), local election commissions, and polling boards oversee the
election.^[71] <#cite_note-:3-71> Seats are allocated using the d'Hondt
method <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/D%27Hondt_method> with an
electoral threshold of 3 percent of all votes cast, although the
threshold is waived for ethnic minority parties.^[67] <#cite_note-:
02-67> ^[72] <#cite_note-72> The seats are distributed by dividing the
total number of votes received by the electoral list participating in
the distribution of seats by each number from one to 250.^[71]
<#cite_note-:3-71> If two or more electoral lists receive the same
quotients on the basis of which the seat is distributed, the electoral
list that received the greater number of votes has priority.^[71]
<#cite_note-:3-71> Parliamentary seats are awarded to candidates from
electoral lists according to their order, starting with the first
candidate from an electoral list.^[68] <#cite_note-:0-68>

A parliamentary election is called by the president of Serbia, who also
has to announce its date and dissolve the National Assembly in the
process.^[68] <#cite_note-:0-68> ^[71] <#cite_note-:3-71> According to
law, the next parliamentary election was supposed to take place by 30
April 2026.^[73] <#cite_note-:4-73> It is possible for a snap election
to take place.^[74] <#cite_note-74> To vote, a person has to be a
citizen and resident of Serbia and at least 18 years old.^[73]
<#cite_note-:4-73> For those who live abroad, they are able to vote at
diplomatic missions.^[67] <#cite_note-:02-67> At least five days before
the election, citizens are notified about the election; citizens receive
information about the day and time of the election and the address of
the polling station where they can vote.^[68] <#cite_note-:0-68>
Election silence <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Election_silence> begins
two days before the voting day and it lasts until the closure of all
polling stations.^[75] <#cite_note-:30-75> During the election day,
eligible voters could vote from 07:00 (UTC+01:00 <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UTC%2B01:00>) to 20:00, though if the polling
station is opened later than 07:00, voting is then extended by the
amount of time for which the opening of the polling station was
delayed.^[71] <#cite_note-:3-71> ^[76] <#cite_note-:33-76> Voters who
are not able to vote at polling stations due to being sick, old, or
invalid have to inform their election commission before 11:00 so that
they could vote on the election day from their home between after
11:00.^[76] <#cite_note-:33-76>


      Election date

In April 2023, newspaper /Danas <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Danas_(newspaper)>/ reported that a parliamentary election, local
elections <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/2023_Serbian_local_elections>,
the Vojvodina provincial election <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
2023_Vojvodina_provincial_election>, and the Belgrade City Assembly
election <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
2023_Belgrade_City_Assembly_election> could be held as early as in
November 2023.^[77] <#cite_note-77> Days after the beginning of the 2023
protests, Vučić said that snap elections could take place before
September 2023.^[78] <#cite_note-78> ^[79] <#cite_note-79> A month
later, Vučić said that "now it is clear that we will have early
parliamentary elections, it is only a matter of time when they will be
held".^[80] <#cite_note-80> At a press conference in July 2023, Vučić
said that an early election "could take place in September or December
if the opposition parties agree. And if not, we will have general
elections in April or May 2024, to be held concurrently with the
Vojvodina provincial election and the regular local elections".^[81]
<#cite_note-81> By law, the Vojvodina provincial election and regular
local elections could be held as late as 30 June 2024.^[82]
<#cite_note-82> A month later, in August, Vučić said that provincial and
local elections will "certainly be held in the next six or seven
months", and that "most likely, parliamentary ones will be held as
well".^[83] <#cite_note-83>

Newspaper /Nova <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nova_(Serbia)>/ and news
portal /N1 <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/N1_(TV_channel)>/ reported in
September 2023 that local, provincial, and parliamentary elections could
be held as early as 19 December 2023.^[84] <#cite_note-84> ^[85]
<#cite_note-85> Vučić and Vučević also held a gathering with officials
from Vojvodina on 5 September.^[86] <#cite_note-86> In late September,
Vučić announced that the elections will be called in December 2023 for
them to be organised on 4 March 2024.^[87] <#cite_note-87> He
subsequently announced, first in September and again in October, that
the elections could be held on 17 December 2023.^[88] <#cite_note-88>
^[89] <#cite_note-89> The decision to hold snap elections was confirmed
on 28 September at a government session.^[90] <#cite_note-90> For the
election to take place on 17 December, the government of Serbia had to
formally propose to dissolve the National Assembly between 18 October
and 2 November.^[91] <#cite_note-:26-91> The president of Serbia then
had 72 hours to decide whether to dissolve the National Assembly or
not.^[91] <#cite_note-:26-91> However, the official campaign period
would only last 45 days if the election is to be called on 2
November.^[92] <#cite_note-:25-92> The government of Serbia sent Vučić a
proposal to dissolve the National Assembly and organise local elections
in 65 cities and municipalities on 30 October.^[93] <#cite_note-93> On 1
November, Vučić dissolved the National Assembly and called the elections
for 17 December.^[94] <#cite_note-94> The official campaign period will
last 46 days.^[95] <#cite_note-95> Ognjen Radonjić, a professor at the
Faculty of Philosophy <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Faculty_of_Philosophy,_University_of_Belgrade>, and Dragan Popović, the
director of the Centre for Practical Politics, argued that the Banjska
attack was one of the main reasons why Vučić announced early
elections.^[96] <#cite_note-96> The Assembly of Vojvodina <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Assembly_of_Vojvodina> also dissolved itself on 16
November, also setting the provincial election date for 17
December.^[97] <#cite_note-97>

The 2023 parliamentary election was the first parliamentary election
since the 2007 parliamentary election <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
2007_Serbian_parliamentary_election>, which was held on 21 January, to
be held in the winter <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Winter>
season.^[98] <#cite_note-98> Dejan Bursać, a teaching associate at the
Institute for Political Studies <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Institute_for_Political_Studies_in_Belgrade>, argued that ruling parties
"avoid the winter for voting because the voters are more dissatisfied,
the turnout cannot be easily controlled because of the weather, and the
costs to the people are increased".^[99] <#cite_note-:06-99> Ivana
Petronijević Terzić, a Demostat journalist, has also listed increased
inflation, higher bills due to heating, and worse weather conditions as
one of the reasons, but she has also said that under "normal
circumstances and a democratic atmosphere" these reasons would be a
problem.^[99] <#cite_note-:06-99> In preparations for the elections, RIK
began acquiring election equipment, including ballot boxes, UV lamps
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/UV_lamps>, and finger sprays, on 13
October.^[100] <#cite_note-100>


      Election conditions

A group of opposition parties, led by the Forward to Europe <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forward_to_Europe> (PE) and Green–Left Front
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Green%E2%80%93Left_Front> (ZLF)
parliamentary groups, sent a joint letter to Organization for Security
and Co-operation in Europe <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Organization_for_Security_and_Co-operation_in_Europe> (OSCE) and other
election observers in Serbia in September 2023, with the request to
declare the election conditions for the upcoming elections.^[101]
<#cite_note-:28-101> PE and ZLF alleged that the conditions have
worsened since the 2022 parliamentary election, while adding that the
organisations should "consider them unfree and undemocratic if they are
held without the recommendations from the Office for Democratic
Institutions and Human Rights <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Office_for_Democratic_Institutions_and_Human_Rights> (ODIHR)
report.^[101] <#cite_note-:28-101> In an /Insajder/ interview, political
scientist Cvijetin Milivojević <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=Cvijetin_Milivojevi%C4%87&action=edit&redlink=1> [sr <https://
sr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cvijetin_Milivojevi%C4%87>] also claimed that the
conditions are "maybe even worse now", but added that due to the short
campaign timespan "it is impossible to fix those conditions now".^[102]
<#cite_note-102> Bursać and Jovana Đurbabić, from non-governmental and
monitoring organisation CRTA <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=CRTA_(organisation)&action=edit&redlink=1> [sr <https://
sr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crta_(organizacija)>], listed "unequal access to
the media, campaign of party officials, and control of the elections at
the polling stations" as three main problems of the election conditions
in Serbia.^[103] <#cite_note-103>

After PE and ZLF, Brnabić also called OSCE and ODIHR in October 2023 to
monitor the elections, while claiming that opposition parties are
"frivolous and are trying to involve foreign factors in causing chaos"
and that they are "trying to form a perception that there is no
framework for free and democratic elections".^[104] <#cite_note-104>
^[105] <#cite_note-105> ^[106] <#cite_note-106> Brnabić claimed that "79
percent of ODIHR's recommendations have been implemented" and that
election conditions have been improved.^[107] <#cite_note-107> ^[108]
<#cite_note-:29-108> In its analysis, CRTA claimed that conditions have
been only improved in administrative aspects and that recommendations
related to voter pressure, misuse of public resources, and media
coverage remain unfulfilled.^[108] <#cite_note-:29-108> DS sent another
request to OSCE in October 2023.^[109] <#cite_note-109> ODIHR announced
that it would monitor the elections and that it would deploy 30 long-
term observers and 250 observers that would observe the elections on the
election day.^[110] <#cite_note-110> The National Youth Council of
Serbia (KOMS) and a delegation of the Parliamentary Assembly of the
Council of Europe <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Parliamentary_Assembly_of_the_Council_of_Europe> (PACE) announced that
they would monitor the elections in Serbia.^[111] <#cite_note-111>
^[112] <#cite_note-112> RIK announced that 5,587 observers would monitor
the elections.^[113] <#cite_note-113>

The Committee for Culture and Information of the National Assembly
amended the ban on campaign of party officials, extending it to 30 days,
as part of the proposed changes to the Law on Public Information and
Media which the National Assembly adopted on 26 October.^[114]
<#cite_note-114> ^[115] <#cite_note-115> On the same day, the National
Assembly also appointed general manager of the National Theatre
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Theatre_in_Belgrade> Svetislav
Goncić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Svetislav_Gonci%C4%87>, political
scientist Aleksandar Milosavljević, professor Dragan Vučinić, political
scientist Jovanka Matić, science advisor Slobodan Prvanović, professor
Branko Rakić, law counselor Miodrag Savović, professor Bojan Tubić,
scientist Vojin Vučićević, and opera singer Aleksandar Stamatović as
members of the supervisory board for the Election Campaign, which was
established on 13 November.^[116] <#cite_note-116> ^[117] <#cite_note-117>

In mid November 2023, ODIHR also announced that it requested to collect
the votes of Serbian citizens on Kosovo.^[118] <#cite_note-118> Kurti
responded by saying that "Kosovo and Serbia must reach a special
agreement so that members of the Serbian community in Kosovo, who have
dual citizenship, could vote on the territory of Kosovo in the Serbian
parliamentary elections".^[119] <#cite_note-119> On 23 November, RIK
announced that there are no conditions to organise elections on Kosovo
and that those with dual citizenship could exercise their right to vote
in Vranje <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vranje>, Kuršumlija <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kur%C5%A1umlija>, Raška <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Ra%C5%A1ka,_Serbia>, and Tutin <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Tutin,_Serbia>.^[120] <#cite_note-120> The European Union criticised
Kosovo's rejection of holding the Serbian parliamentary election on its
territory.^[121] <#cite_note-121>

Trnovac <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Veliki_Trnovac>, where a seat in
the National Assembly was battled between SPS and the Albanian Coalition

enough votes to earn a seat in the National Assembly.^[125]

      Pre-election composition

The thirteenth convocation of the National Assembly <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/13th_National_Assembly_of_Serbia> held its first
session on 1 August 2022, at which the current parliamentary composition
was formalised.^[126] <#cite_note-126> ^[127] <#cite_note-:7-127>
Vladimir Orlić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_Orli%C4%87> of
SNS was elected president of the National Assembly <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/President_of_the_National_Assembly_of_Serbia> on 2
August, while Sandra Božić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Sandra_Bo%C5%BEi%C4%87> (SNS), Snežana Paunović <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sne%C5%BEana_Paunovi%C4%87> (SPS), Elvira Kovács
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Elvira_Kov%C3%A1cs> (Alliance of
Vojvodina Hungarians <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Alliance_of_Vojvodina_Hungarians>, VMSZ), Usame Zukorlić <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Usame_Zukorli%C4%87> (Justice and Reconciliation
Party <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Justice_and_Reconciliation_Party>,
SPP), Božidar Delić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Bo%C5%BEidar_Deli%C4%87> (National Democratic Alternative <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Democratic_Alternative_(Serbia)>, NADA),
Borko Stefanović <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Borko_Stefanovi%C4%87>
(SPP), and Zoran Lutovac <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zoran_Lutovac>
(DS) were elected vice-presidents.^[128] <#cite_note-128> Delić died
shortly after taking office and was replaced by Vojislav Mihailović
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vojislav_Mihailovi%C4%87> in November
2022.^[129] <#cite_note-129> ^[130] <#cite_note-130> The composition of
the National Assembly has changed since the first session in August
2022; for example, the European Regions <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
European_Regions_(Serbia)>, a parliamentary group composed of minority
interest parties, briefly existed until October 2022.^[131]
<#cite_note-131> ^[132] <#cite_note-132> The last session of the
thirteenth convocation was held from 23 to 26 October.^[133]
<#cite_note-133> ^[134] <#cite_note-134>

    Pre-election activities


      Government parties

Vučić hinted at a potential formation of a bloc or a movement in
September 2022, which he later revealed in March 2023 to be the People's
Movement for the State <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
People%27s_Movement_for_the_State> (NPZD).^[136] <#cite_note-:14-136>
^[137] <#cite_note-137> He said that NPZD would act as a "supra-party
movement" and that SNS would not be dissolved.^[138] <#cite_note-138>
Vučević confirmed that SNS would take part in the movement.^[139]
<#cite_note-:10-139> ^[140] <#cite_note-:11-140> Initially announced to
be formalised in May 2023, the date was subsequently moved towards
autumn 2023.^[141] <#cite_note-:102-141> ^[142] <#cite_note-142> As of
October 2023, the movement has not yet been formed.^[143] <#cite_note-:
18-143> Political commentators have pointed out that SPS is divided on
the issue of joining NPZD and that as a result, the formalisation of
NPZD was delayed.^[143] <#cite_note-:18-143> Predrag J. Marković
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Predrag_J._Markovi%C4%87>, one of the
vice-presidents of SPS, has said that "SPS would lose its identity if it
joins [NPZD]".^[144] <#cite_note-144> Bojan Klačar, the executive
director of CeSID <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CeSID>, has said that
with the September 2023 announcement that the elections would be held on
17 December, "the creation of the Movement could be risky".^[145]
<#cite_note-145> Considering that NPZD was not mentioned at the main
board session of SNS in October 2023, Đorđe Vukadinović <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/%C4%90or%C4%91e_Vukadinovi%C4%87> of /Nova srpska
politička misao <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Nova_srpska_politi%C4%8Dka_misao>/ (NSPM) and political analyst Dragomir
Anđelković argued that NPZD could be used only as a slogan for the
upcoming elections.^[146] <#cite_note-146> Vučić, however, announced in
late October 2023 that NPZD will be formed "in the coming period".^[147]
<#cite_note-147> Healthy Serbia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Healthy_Serbia>, led by Milan Stamatović <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Milan_Stamatovi%C4%87>, was announced as an addition to the SNS-led
coalition.^[148] <#cite_note-148>

Tomislav Žigmanov <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Tomislav_%C5%BDigmanov>, the president of the Democratic Alliance of
Croats in Vojvodina <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Democratic_Alliance_of_Croats_in_Vojvodina> (DSHV), announced in October
2023 that DSHV would contest the 2023 election in an electoral alliance
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electoral_alliance>, with the
justification being "because minorities in Serbia do not have guaranteed
seats in the parliament".^[149] <#cite_note-149> Rasim Ljajić <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rasim_Ljaji%C4%87>, the president of the Social
Democratic Party of Serbia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Social_Democratic_Party_of_Serbia> (SDPS), announced that SDPS will
contest the elections with SNS again.^[150] <#cite_note-150> The Party
for Sandžak <https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=Party_for_Sand%C5%BEak&action=edit&redlink=1>, aligned with Ljajić
since 2004, announced that it would contest the elections alone and not
with SDPS.^[151] <#cite_note-151> Additionally, SNS and SPS announced
that they would not contest the parliamentary election together and that
SPS would again take part in the election JS and Greens of Serbia
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greens_of_Serbia> (ZS).^[152]
<#cite_note-152> ^[153] <#cite_note-153>


      Opposition parties

After the dissolution of the UZPS coalition, SSP, Movement of Free
Citizens <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Movement_of_Free_Citizens_(Serbia)>, Movement for Reversal <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Movement_for_Reversal>, and United Trade Unions of
Serbia Sloga <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
United_Trade_Unions_of_Serbia_%22Sloga%22>, formed the Ujedinjeni
parliamentary group in the National Assembly in August 2022.^[14]
<#cite_note-:1-14> ^[154] <#cite_note-154> Ujedinjeni changed its name
to Forward to Europe in July 2023.^[155] <#cite_note-155> Zdravko Ponoš
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Zdravko_Pono%C5%A1>, the presidential
candidate of the UPZS coalition, left Narodna after the 2022
elections.^[156] <#cite_note-156> He formed the Serbia Centre <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serbia_Centre> (SRCE) organisation in June 2022
and in July 2023 it was registered as a political party.^[157]
<#cite_note-:12-157> ^[158] <#cite_note-158> The Movement of Free
Serbia, which was also a member of the UZPS coalition, merged into DS in
September 2022.^[159] <#cite_note-159>

The We Must <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/We_Must_(Serbia)> alliance
was also dissolved after the 2022 elections, but its member parties said
that they would continue cooperating in the National Assembly.^[127]
<#cite_note-:7-127> ^[160] <#cite_note-160> Together, with Aleksandar
Jovanović Ćuta <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Aleksandar_Jovanovi%C4%87_%C4%86uta>, Biljana Stojković <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biljana_Stojkovi%C4%87>, and Nebojša Zelenović
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neboj%C5%A1a_Zelenovi%C4%87> as its co-
presidents, was formed in June 2022 as a merger of Together for Serbia
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Together_for_Serbia>, Ecological Uprising
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ecological_Uprising> (EU), and Assembly
of Free Serbia.^[161] <#cite_note-161> ^[162] <#cite_note-:03-162>
Solidarity, which was also affiliated with the We Must alliance, merged
into Together in January 2023.^[163] <#cite_note-163> In August 2023,
Ćuta and other members of EU left Together and joined the parliamentary
group led by People's Movement of Serbia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
People%27s_Movement_of_Serbia> (NPS).^[164] <#cite_note-164> ^[165]
<#cite_note-165> NDB announced in late June 2022 that it had adopted a
platform to work on becoming a registered political party; it began
collecting signatures in May 2023.^[166] <#cite_note-:04-166> ^[167]
<#cite_note-167> NDB also announced that it would rename itself to
Green–Left Front (ZLF).^[168] <#cite_note-:13-168> ZLF was formalised in
July 2023.^[169] <#cite_note-:52-169>

Within Narodna, a dispute between its president Vuk Jeremić <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vuk_Jeremi%C4%87> and vice-president Miroslav
Aleksić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Miroslav_Aleksi%C4%87_(born_1978)> began in June 2023.^[170]
<#cite_note-170> Aleksić was eventually removed from the position of the
party's executive board in July 2023; he then publicly acknowledged the
conflict between him and Jeremić.^[171] <#cite_note-171> ^[172]
<#cite_note-172> Although a leadership election was scheduled for
October 2023, Aleksić left Narodna and reconstituted NPS in August
2023.^[173] <#cite_note-173> Together with Miloš Parandilović <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milo%C5%A1_Parandilovi%C4%87>, the president of
the New Face of Serbia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
New_Face_of_Serbia> (NLS), they formed a parliamentary group in the
National Assembly.^[174] <#cite_note-174>

Following his dismissal from JS, Basta formed the European Way Movement
<https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?
title=European_Way_Movement&action=edit&redlink=1> (PEP), now portraying
himself as an opposition politician.^[175] <#cite_note-175> ^[176]
<#cite_note-:27-176> Mladen Mrdalj, a political scientist, criticised
Basta, describing him as an opportunist <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Political_opportunism>.^[176] <#cite_note-:27-176> PEP announced in
September 2023 that it would contest the upcoming elections.^[177]
<#cite_note-177> Besides Basta, former SNS-turned-opposition politicians
Zorana Mihajlović <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Zorana_Mihajlovi%C4%87>, Dragan Šormaz <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Dragan_%C5%A0ormaz>, and Stanislava Pak <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Stanislava_Pak> formed the Always for Serbia <https://en.wikipedia.org/
w/index.php?title=Always_for_Serbia&action=edit&redlink=1> organisation
in September 2023.^[178] <#cite_note-178> Opposition parties organising
the Serbia Against Violence protests refused to cooperate with them due
to formerly being aligned with SNS.^[179] <#cite_note-179>


      Election alliances

Borislav Novaković, Biljana Đorđević, Đorđe Pavićević, Robert Kozma,
Zoran Lutovac, Dragan Rakić, Marinika Tepić, Radomir Lazović, Nebojša
Zelenović, Pavle Grbović, Srđan Milivojević, Miroslav Aleksić, Đorđe
Miketić, Borko Stefanović, Željko Veselinović, and Janko Veselinović at
a press conference inside the National Assembly of Serbia in September
2023 <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Dogovor_za_pobedu_(cropped).jpg>
Opposition parties organising the Serbia Against Violence protests
(representatives pictured) formed a joint coalition in October 2023

In an interview for newspaper /Nova <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Nova_(Serbia)>/ in August 2023, Marinika Tepić <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marinika_Tepi%C4%87>, one of the vice-presidents
of SSP, stated that the creation of an electoral alliance of political
parties that organise the Serbia Against Violence protests is
possible.^[180] <#cite_note-:15-180> "During this one year of work in
the National Assembly, and especially in these last three months during
the protests, we, [opposition parties], managed to build trust stronger
than ever before", Tepić said.^[180] <#cite_note-:15-180> It has been
noted that DS, SRCE, Together, and ZLF opposition parties have
intensively cooperated with each other, as early as April 2023.^[181]
<#cite_note-:16-181> ^[182] <#cite_note-182> DS, SRCE, and Together
signed a cooperation agreement in late August 2023, establishing greater
cooperation between the parties.^[183] <#cite_note-183> ^[184]
<#cite_note-184> Slobodan Cvejić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Slobodan_Cveji%C4%87> of SRCE added that "there is room for greater
coalition cooperation" between opposition parties.^[185] <#cite_note-185>

Radomir Lazović <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radomir_Lazovi%C4%87> of
ZLF has also announced in July 2023 that ZLF prepared framework
principles of cooperation to achieve non-aggression among opposition
parties, joint control of elections, and consolidation of parties into a
bigger alliance.^[186] <#cite_note-186> Lazović has, however, opposed
the creation of a single coalition of all opposition parties, stating
that it could possibly lead to the loss of votes.^[187] <#cite_note-187>
PE member parties, ZLF, DS, Together, NPS, EU, and NLS signed an
agreement, titled Agreement for Victory, in late September 2023,
announcing joint support for calling snap elections and electoral
reforms.^[188] <#cite_note-188> Since then, the parties that signed the
agreement have held meetings on discussing the creation of a joint
electoral alliance; /N1/ reported that the distribution of seats among
parties in the alliance is "the biggest obstacle to the formation of a
single alliance".^[189] <#cite_note-189> ^[190] <#cite_note-190> At a 18
October press conference, Srđan Milivojević <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Sr%C4%91an_Milivojevi%C4%87> of DS, said that there were "no
dissonant voices" at the discussions about the creation of a joint
Serbia Against Violence alliance.^[191] <#cite_note-191> Nova–DS2P
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Party_(Serbia)> and the Social
Democratic Party <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Social_Democratic_Party_(Serbia)> (SDS), led by Boris Tadić <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Boris_Tadi%C4%87>, both expressed support for
joining the Serbia Against Violence alliance.^[192] <#cite_note-192>
^[193] <#cite_note-193> The agreement between the parties was reached on
26 October and the coalition, named Serbia Against Violence <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serbia_Against_Violence_(coalition)> (SPN), was
formalised and presented to the public a day later.^[194] <#cite_note-:
39-194> ^[195] <#cite_note-195>

Aleksić has also proposed the formation of a "stronger, wider bloc" for
the upcoming elections; /Nova/ also reported that SRCE and Nova–DS2P
could take part in such an electoral alliance.^[196] <#cite_note-196>
^[197] <#cite_note-197> SDS held negotiations with Aleksić's NPS in
October 2023 about the formation of a joint coalition.^[198]
<#cite_note-198> Parandilović of NLS has also expressed his support for
the creation of a wide electoral alliance; Aleksandar Olenik <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aleksandar_Olenik>, a representative of Together
for Vojvodina <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Together_for_Vojvodina_(party)> in the National Assembly, on the other
hand, has said that opposition parties should run on two electoral lists
instead.^[181] <#cite_note-:16-181> Ćuta has also expressed his support
for the creation of an alliance that would include opposition parties
from the left and right.^[199] <#cite_note-199> Milica Đurđević
Stamenkovski <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Milica_%C4%90ur%C4%91evi%C4%87_Stamenkovski>, the president of the
Serbian Party Oathkeepers <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Serbian_Party_Oathkeepers> (SSZ), expressed her opposition towards
creating a joint opposition alliance due to foreign policy
differences.^[200] <#cite_note-200>

Boško Obradović <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Bo%C5%A1ko_Obradovi%C4%87>, the president of Dveri <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dveri>, has also voiced his support for the
formation of a "state-building bloc" that would include SSZ and National
Democratic Alternative <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
National_Democratic_Alternative_(Serbia)> (NADA); Obradović also added
that he also sees Narodna as a member in their bloc.^[201]
<#cite_note-201> Miloš Jovanović <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Milo%C5%A1_Jovanovi%C4%87>, the president of the New Democratic Party of
Serbia <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_Democratic_Party_of_Serbia>
(NDSS), stated that NADA would be open to cooperate with Dveri and SSZ,
while Jeremić has called for the formation of a coalition that would
include Dveri, SSZ, NADA, and Enough is Enough <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Enough_is_Enough_(party)> (DJB).^[202]
<#cite_note-202> ^[203] <#cite_note-203> In October 2023, Dveri and SSZ
formalised a coalition, named National Gathering <https://
en.wikipedia.org/wiki/National_Gathering_(Serbia)> (NO), for the
upcoming elections; they also invited NADA and Narodna to join their
bloc.^[204] <#cite_note-:17-204> NADA, however, held negotiations with
Narodna instead.^[205] <#cite_note-205> ^[206] <#cite_note-206>
Negotiations between Dveri, SSZ, NADA, and Narodna were held in late
October 2023, however, the parties were unable to form a joint electoral
alliance due to SSZ rejecting the proposed document.^[207]
<#cite_note-207> ^[208] <#cite_note-208> Narodna and the Serbian Radical
Party <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serbian_Radical_Party>, led by
Vojislav Šešelj <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Vojislav_%C5%A0e%C5%A1elj>, opted to take part in the elections
independently.^[209] <#cite_note-209> ^[210] <#cite_note-210> DJB
initially announced that they would contest the elections independently,
saying that "there are no serious talks of forming one state-building
coalition".^[211] <#cite_note-211>


    Electoral lists

With the dissolution of the National Assembly and a call for elections
to be held on 17 December, the deadline to submit electoral lists was
set for 26 November.^[212] <#cite_note-212> The collective electoral
list was published by RIK on 1 December, with 2,818 candidates in
total.^[213] <#cite_note-213> The following table includes electoral
lists that were confirmed by RIK and that took part in the 2023
parliamentary election.^[214] <#cite_note-214> The electoral lists of
PEP, which submitted its electoral list under two Bunjevci minority
parties, and Pavle Bihali <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pavle_Bihali>
of Levijatan <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leviathan_Movement>-led
Russian Minority Alliance were rejected by RIK due to not having enough
valid signatures.^[215] <#cite_note-215> ^[216] <#cite_note-
RIK_ALBANCI2-216> ^[217] <#cite_note-217>

list/

    Campaign

Croatian daily newspaper /Večernji list <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Ve%C4%8Dernji_list>/ has described the 2023 election as "the most
uncertain election since 2012".^[234] <#cite_note-235> Political
scientist and journalist Aleksandar Ivković argued that the campaign has
been "dirty", listing graffitis, posters, music videos, and tabloid
media content that were perpetrated against opposition parties.^[235]
<#cite_note-:42-236> "While the content is largely unchanged, it seems
that SNS is now really 'firing from all guns blazing' when it comes to
negative campaigning against the opposition, which was not the case
before, at least not to such an extent", Ivković has said.^[235]
<#cite_note-:42-236> Political scientist Boban Stojanović estimated that
"political tensions are not decreasing" and that "political violence
will be seen until the end of the election campaign".^[236]
<#cite_note-237> Stojanović also added that the 2023 "elections are even
more uncertain than the previous ones".^[237] <#cite_note-238>

During its visit in Serbia, PACE representatives concluded that the
campaign was met with polarisation <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Political_polarization> and intimidation from the government.^[238]
<#cite_note-239> Raša Nedeljkov from CRTA said that he expects "an
intense and dirty campaign in which hate speech will be the backbone of
the campaign"; he noted the targeting of political opponents, media
representatives, civil society organisations, and election observers as
examples.^[239] <#cite_note-240> CRTA claimed that voter intimidation
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Voter_intimidation> has increased during
the campaign period, stating that in its research "door-to-door
campaigning is one of the basic activities as a form of direct,
immediate communication between parties and voters".^[240]
<#cite_note-241> During the first three weeks of the election campaign,
CRTA reported in its analysis that "inequality, suspicions and threats"
were seen, claiming that government parties had significant advantage
due to the abuse of institutions.^[241] <#cite_note-242> Amidst the
campaign, the Centre for Investigative Journalism of Serbia (CINS) also
released a report in which it was revealed that during the campaign
period an organisation operating a call centre was tasked with asking
voters whether they would vote for SNS or not.^[242] <#cite_note-:
43-243> CINS also reported that the call centre also possessed a
database of voters, with voters sorted in groups based on whether they
are a member of SNS, a SNS voter, or a non-SNS voter.^[242]
<#cite_note-:43-243>

"It's Up To Us Too", an informal organisation of high school students,
launched a campaign in October 2023 named "It's Up To You Too" (/I ti se
pitaš/), aimed at first-time voters in the 2023 parliamentary
election.^[243] <#cite_note-244> The Civic Initiatives, a non-
governmental organisation, also launched a campaign with activists,
professors, and public figures to boost the turnout and the number of
controllers (members of polling stations) in the 2023 election.^[244]
<#cite_note-245> A group of intellectuals, including actors Svetlana
Bojković <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Svetlana_Bojkovi%C4%87>, Dragan
Bjelogrlić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dragan_Bjelogrli%C4%87>,
former Serbian Academy of Sciences and Arts <https://en.wikipedia.org/
wiki/Serbian_Academy_of_Sciences_and_Arts> president Vladimir S. Kostić
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vladimir_S._Kosti%C4%87>, legal scholar
and judge Miodrag Majić <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Miodrag_Maji%C4%87>, and former University of Belgrade rector Ivanka
Popović <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ivanka_Popovi%C4%87>, formed the
initiative "ProClaim" on 7 November 2023 to boost the electoral turnout
for the 2023 election and "stop the explosion of crime and corruption in
society".^[245] <#cite_note-246> The ProGlas initiative received than
more 100,000 signatures of support by 15 November.^[246]
<#cite_note-247> Regarding ProGlas, political scientist Duško
Radosavljević said that "if we will remember this campaign for
something, it will be because of the attempt by professional and
respected people to ask for a new understanding of politics. For me,
that is a force that provides a real chance for Serbia to be
better".^[247] <#cite_note-248>

The 2023 parliamentary election campaign also saw the increase of usage
of the social media platform TikTok <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
TikTok> among politicians.^[248] <#cite_note-:44-249> ^[249]
<#cite_note-250> Vučić, Tadić, Tepić, SPS president Ivica Dačić
<https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ivica_Da%C4%8Di%C4%87>, and finance
minister Siniša Mali <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sini%C5%A1a_Mali>
all joined the platform in November 2023, in order to present themselves
to younger voters.^[248] <#cite_note-:44-249> ^[250] <#cite_note-251>
Jelena Kleut, a professor at the Faculty of Philosophy of the University
of Belgrade <https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/
Faculty_of_Philosophy,_University_of_Belgrade>, said that "knowing the
Serbian Progressive Party, this move is based on data suggesting that
the TikTok electorate might be interested in going to the polls in the
election".^[248] <#cite_note-:44-249>
